♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ 0 ♥ "Vima Kadphises, Kanishka and other Kushan emperors could undertake military ventures in distant lands for months at a stretch without a thought ever crossing their minds that someone within their family or within their clan would undercut them and usurp their throne. Transition of rule from one emperor to another was always smooth."[481]
♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ 0 ♥ "Vima Kadphises, Kanishka and other Kushan emperors could undertake military ventures in distant lands for months at a stretch without a thought ever crossing their minds that someone within their family or within their clan would undercut them and usurp their throne. Transition of rule from one emperor to another was always smooth."[482]

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ inferred present ♥

The administrative base was moved from Bactria to Gandhara once the latter region was conquered and it remained there until the end of the empire.[483] However, the precise location was adjusted within Gandhara for strategic reasons. Kujula Kadphises administrative base was at Takshasila-Sirkap. As the empire grew Vima Kadphises built a new capital and larger administrative center at Taxila-Sirsukh.[484] Kanishka moved the capital again to Purushapura, which was closer to the Khyber Pass, and troublesome Bactria.[485]. Other "Major administrative centers are believed to have been Peshawar in northwest Pakistan and Mathura in the Ganges Basin."[486] Government likely had three main levels, central administration, provincial and local government.[487]

Little or no literature specifically covers the central government: was it a court bureaucracy; did a secular administrative institution even exist independent of the Buddhist hierarchy? The state chancery used both "Bactrian written in Greek script and Gandhari written in Kharoshthi".[488] There also was a "formulae transmitted from the Achaemenians."[489]

In theory "The king seems to have possessed unfettered powers, as we find no reference in the Kushan records to any advisory body or to councillors corresponding to amatyas and sachivas of the Mauryan period."[490] In practice "The Kushans were great conquerors but poor administrators."[491]

The king may well have been completely reliant on local populations and their own laws for the day-to-day running of his administrations, both at the Kushan capital and in the regions. The stable administration of the capital in Gandhara certainly required the compliance of the powerful Buddhist establishment.[492] The central importance of this Buddhist establishment is shown by the Buddhist scholars who were close advisors to the Kushan Emperors[493] and in the running of Gandhara itself (the core Kushan region) which required administrative support from many of the "20,000 Buddhist monks working in the Buddhist institutions".[494]

Outside Gandhara, the Kushans "did not possess the political acumen or the administrative capability to maintain control over the conquered territories."[495] Nevertheless "The epithets appended to the names of the Kushana kings in both inscriptions and coins illustrate the rulers' success in asserting universal sovereignty, [which] include maharaja (great king), rajadiraja (king of kings), kaisara (Caesar) (Shrava 1993:159), and devaputra (son of god).[496] In the regions, rule was likely through indigenous institutions through vassal kings or appointed trustworthy leaders, with Satraps known.[497]

Kushans never set up regional leaders as powerful governors with military forces (like Sasanian Satraps) and their regional leaders never had an effective link with the Kushan capital.[498] Therefore, the major Kushan administrative centers outside at "Mathura in the Ganges Basin",[499] Begram[500] in Afghanistan and at Taxila[501] after the capital had been moved to Purushapura[502] - both in northern Pakistan - all likely had a large degree of autonomy from the ruling Kushans (although some homogenization in Gandhara and Bactria e.g. in administrative script might be inferred through Buddhist lines of authority).

The lack of Kushan interest in an homogenous system of law across their empire, and no central administrative institutions such as a council of nobles where such a system might be discussed, might be considered confirmed by the fact they "saw nothing strange in simultaneously worshipping deities of the Greek, Perso-Mesopotamian, Zoroastrian and Hindu pantheon."[503] Kushan rulers were conquerors who ruled only through their military prowess and donations to religious authorities, both which is expressed on highly standardized[504] (but fabulously hereogenous in design[505]) Kushan coinage.

The military power "made the Silk Routes possible"[506] and the Kushan monarchy rich because it "enabled them to have a share in the profits in the lucrative trade between Rome and China."[507] The immense wealth then passed from the Kushan establishment to the indigenous religious authorities in the form of donations and patronage[508][509]. It was through the educated elites of these religious authorities the administration, law and justice systems of a largely decentralized Kushian empire was run.

The fact the capital was frequently moved[510] suggests there was some kind of central authority that was important for the king to remain in contact with. "Titles on coins and inscriptions record the existence of numerous administrative ranks or offices".[511] Standard scribal tradition across the Kushan territory (Central Asia, India, Afghanistan) suggests a coherent administrative structure[512] but this could be referring to Buddhist administrators.

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ lower ♥ Prestige lower to non-royal nobility. Buddhists (non-royal nobility) were an essential for the administration in Gandhara.[513] Do not have evidence for a large secular bureaucracy.

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ suspected unknown ♥

"The Kushans were great conquerors but poor administrators."[530] and the stable administration of the capital was to a large degree reliant on the compliance of the Buddhist establishment.[531] However, outside the capital "in Bactria, or Sogdiana or in Tajikistan or Turkmenistan or the Tarim Basin or in the Jumma Basin" they needed another approach for controlling the regions.[532] Generally this meant intimidation and military force because "they did not possess the political acumen or the adminsitrative capability to maintain control over the conquered territories."[533] For example, although Kujula Kadphises conquered Greater Bactria and left trusted men in charge "there is no evidence to suggest that he provided his representatives with sufficient military troops, or established an effective link with his own organization in Taxila."[534] Thus when kingdoms could not be ruled as vassals (like Kashgar during the reign of Kanishka and Mathura during the reigns of Huvishka and Vasudeva)[535] local uprisings occurred.[536] Kanishka may have moved the capital to Purushapura because it was a shorter distance from Bactria.[537] Satrapies (powerful governors) who possessed a well-equipped military might have been a more effective system for ruling the regions but "putting in place an effective satrapy system was not within the capabilities of the Kushans."[538]


♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥

India: "Indian cities were administered by a governor. Subordinate to him were the three chief magistrates. The district inspector (gopah) was in charge of ten, twenty or forty families. He was expected to know the caste, names and occupations of all the men and women living in his district, and even how much they earned and spent. There was also a borough inspector (sthanikah) in charge of each of the four city sections. Each of these inspectors managed the affairs of one quarter of the fortified city. (Kautilya Arthastra II.36.1-4)."[539] "In India the municipal authorities controlled the activities of artisans and merchants. There are references to city councils and some cities had a city seal."[540]


Central Asia: "the commune occupied an important place in the socio-economic life of Cenral Asia and in the ancient East as a whole. This seems to have continued until the Early Middle Ages, for which evidence is available. Thus, the commune in Sogdiana was known as naf; it consisted of the aristocracy (azat, azatkar), merchants (xvakar), and free peasants (who were members of the commune) and craftsmen (karikar). Of these three categories in the naf the highest status was enjoyed by the azat, that is, persons of 'high and noble birth', the azatkar or free persons associated with the azat and the 'children of the azat of aristocratic, noble origin.' According to the written sources, the azat owned the land and the villages and were the chief retainers of the local and provincial rulers."[541]

Limited evidence suggests "the Kushan rulers accepted the prevalent Indian and Chinese concept of the divinity of kingship, and borrowed the Achaemenid and subsequently Indo-Greek and Indo-Parthian system of appointing satraps as provincial governors, while the fuedal lord (dandanayaka) was their own creation. The title is no doubt Indian, but all fuedal lords known to have been associated with the Kushan administration were foreigners."[542] "ksatrapas were definitely at a higher administrative level than the dandanayakas" but the relationship between them is not known.[543] "The use of foreigners alone at the higher level of political organization ensured efficiency and minimized the chances of internal dissension and disorder, but this principle was not applied at local village level."[544] i.e. ksatrapas and dandanayakas were foreigners/Kushans.'

Government likely had three main levels, central administration, provincial and local government.[545] Like Greeks and Parthians, ksatrapas and mahaksatrapas appointed to run units of the empire.[546] Inscriptions mention satraps and other officials have been found. "Satraps are known for Kapisa (Begram), Manikyala (near Rawalpindi), Und (west of the Indus), Mathura, Varanasi, etc. There may have been satraps for other parts of the empire, but the evidence on this point is wanting."[547] "The inscriptions mention two terms - 'gramika' and 'padrapala' - both signifying 'village headman', who collected the king's dues and took cognizance of crimes in his area. There is no information about the local government that we find later in the Gupta period."[548]


Authority over local-level administrators

Property Rights[edit]

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ 10% ♥ Previously coded [7-12], presumably meant as a percentage. The Kushan Empire traded enslaved people with South Asia[625] and the Roman Empire.[626] However, it seems that enslaved people were more likely to be employed in urban rather than rural settings,[627] and the "brunt of the task of building irrigation works was, however, borne by the peasants, and many irrigation systems were dug by labourers from the rural communities, without any particular expenditure of effort or contribution by the authorities. [...] In spite of the very considerable number of slaves, slavery was not the only, and probably not the predominant, form of labour. Little use was made of slaves either in agriculture or in handicraft work, as their labour was not profitable."[628]

♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ [5-10] ♥ Free peasants in communes of Central Asia. Land was owned by the azat (aristocrats) but the peasants were free to move away if they wished. Attempts may have been made to attach commune members to the land. Do not know the situation in India. If they had village headmen that might suggest they were not subject to lords.

Central Asia

"There is some evidence to show that communes owned whole irrigation systems and the regions irrigated by them, as well as settlements and grazing lands."[629] "the state and large land-holders, who tried to attach members of the commune to the land - a process that ultimately led to the emergence of feudalism in Central Asia."[630]
"the commune occupied an important place in the socio-economic life of Central Asia and in the ancient East as a whole. This seems to have continued until the Early Middle Ages, for which evidence is available. Thus, the commune in Sogdiana was known as naf; it consisted of the aristocracy (azat, azatkar), merchants (xvakar), and free peasants (who were members of the commune) and craftsmen (karikar). Of these three categories in the naf the highest status was enjoyed by the azat, that is, persons of 'high and noble birth', the azatkar or free persons associated with the azat and the 'children of the azat of aristocratic, noble origin.' According to the written sources, the azat owned the land and the villages and were the chief retainers of the local and provincial rulers."[631]
"The brunt of the task of building irrigation works was, however, borne by the peasants, and many irrigation systems were dug by labourers from the rural communities, without any particular expenditure of effort or contribution by the authorities."[632]


India ?

"The inscriptions mention two terms - 'gramika' and 'padrapala' - both signifying 'village headman', who collected the king's dues and took cognizance of crimes in his area. There is no information about the local government that we find later in the Gupta period."[633]

Religion and Normative Ideology[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni; Edward A L Turner ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Description of the Normative Ideology

"Essentially the Sakas and Kushans who invaded Graeco-Bactria may have had similar religious ideas and cults to the population of Sogdiana and Bactria in pre-Zoroastrian times. They probably worshipped Ahuramazdah as ‘God of Heaven’ with solar features and Svanta Armati as ‘Goddess of Earth’. They were acquainted with several categories of divine beings such as daivas, yazatas and bagas; and used the verb yaz- as a term for sacrifice and worship, and the words baga-spasika- and bagana-pati- to denote different categories of priests. [...] When the Sakas and Kushans conquered the Graeco-Bactrian territories north of the Oxus, they found manifestations of Greek religious life, religious architecture, sculpture, ideas and worship. [...] Apparently the Kushan aristocracy tried to adopt the royal ideology of the Graeco-Bactrian kings and its religious implications. [...] The coinage of the next Kushan ruler, Kujula Kadphises, enables us to see how the religious horizon of the Kushans was enlarged. [...] Contrary to earlier assumptions, which regarded Kujula Kadphises as Buddhist on the basis of this epithet, it is now clear from the wording of a Mathura inscription, in which Huvishka bears the same epithet satyadharmasthita that the kingdom was conferred upon him by Sarva and Scamdavira (Candavıra), that is, he was a devotee of Siva. [...] At the time when Vima Kadphises became Kushan emperor, religious life can be characterized by two interesting features. One is the adoption of the forms of Greek religious art and the Greek iconographic interpretation of Kushan divinities. [...] The other striking feature is the strong orientation towards Indian religions and the worship of Siva in particular. It is, therefore, not surprising that the cult of Siva became even more prominent under Vima Kadphises, who conquered considerable territories in India. [...] While in the interest of his Indian conquest, Vima Kadphises had given preference to the worship of Siva in his religious policy, his successor Kanishka put Bactria and its Iranian religious cults at the centre of his religious policy. [...] None the less, the importance of India and the Indian religions, especially the worship ofSiva, remained unchanged. [...] Kanishka II clearly recognized the importance of Buddhism in his kingdom. [...] None the less, he did not neglect other religions and cults. On the reverse types of coins beside Boddo (Buddha) we find other Iranian divinities both Zoroastrian and local. [...] In spite of the scanty evidence, fragmentary in many respects, we can draw somegen- eral conclusions about religious life throughout the territory of the Kushans. It was highly developed and differentiated. The religious movements of India - Sivaism, Vishnuism, Jainism and Buddhism with their different schools - penetrated Central Asia, as did Indian merchants when Kushan rule facilitated long-distance international trade. In eastern Iran the Indian religions met the Greek divinities, Zoroastrianism and many local pre-Zoroastrian forms of worship, and encountered the ancient Iranian religious ideas of the northern Iranian equestrian nomads. The Kushan kings selected for themselves from this immense variety those religious elements, ideas and forms of cults which fitted their ancient religious traditions and strengthened the religious ideology of their royal power. So the ‘Kushan pantheon’ appearing on the coins represents only a selection of the religious cults of their empire.//None the less, the Kushan kings were well aware of the current trends in religious life and followed them. The most important was syncretism. The great religions influenced one another and began slowly to absorb the local cults." [637]


Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ present ♥ "The Kushan kings derived their royal power from divine patrons, and so they were charismatic kings, human incarnations of divine might and power. As a consequence of their charisma, they also became objects of divine worship in dynastic sanctuaries." [638]

"the Kushan king ascribed his rise to power to Sarva (=́Siva) and Candavira (who may be the same god as Candisvara, the god of the Mahakala temple at Ujjain, probably a special form of́ Siva). Because the circle of gods around́ Siva have a warlike character, it is very likely that the phrase iazado i karisaro 'the warlike divinity' also denoted́ Siva."[639]

"it seems that originally the divine patron of the Kushan dynasty was the ancient Iranian moon god. In view of the close connection between Siva and the moon, dynastic religious ideas may have also suggested to Vima [Kadphises] the choice of Siva as his divine patron. ... the support of the Indian population of his kingdom may have been important for Vima both before and during his Indian campaign."[640]

"A colossal statue of Kaniska near Mathura with a Brahmi inscription labeling him 'Great King, King of Kings, Son of God, Kaniska' shows that he fulfilled the role of 'Universal Emperor' (cakravartin)."[641]

"The divinity of kingship seems to have been the most conspicuous element in the Kushan political system. Their kings were not only accorded the title of 'devaputra' (Son of God), corresponding to the Chinese imperial title "t'ien-tzu" (Son of Heaven), but were deified after death and their statues were set up in a devakula (god house)." [642]

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ absent ♥ Chakravartins were not believed to have supernatural powers [643].

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ {absent; present} ♥

Rulers were incarnations of gods rather than their descendants. "The Kushan kings derived their royal power from divine patrons, and so they were charismatic kings, human incarnations of divine might and power. As a consequence of their charisma, they also became objects of divine worship in dynastic sanctuaries." [644]

"The divinity of kingship seems to have been the most conspicuous element in the Kushan political system. Their kings were not only accorded the title of 'devaputra' (Son of God), corresponding to the Chinese imperial title "t'ien-tzu" (Son of Heaven), but were deified after death and their statues were set up in a devakula (god house)." [645]

Inscription in the Rabatak temple lists gods 'from whom the king as obtained kingship.' Five are Zoroastrian but two are not.[646] Muzhdwan "is portrayed as a Yuezhi rider with a typical Scythian hooded cap, and it seems reasonable to suppose that Kanishka took this personal protector from his own stock of family gods."[647] Terracotta figurines of horsemen with pointy caps are found at Kushan-Bactrian sites and usually assigned to category of 'rider gods' or 'heroized ancestors.'[648] Umma was a goddess ("not to be confused with the Indian goddess Uma who is shown on some later coins") whose Iranian name means 'highest' or 'supreme'. The Rabatak inscription said she was "welcoming in her presence all the other gods named here." Scythians had a goddess called Tabiti (Herodotus) who might fit her description as a "bestower of power".[649] A terracotta of Umma found in a former Yuezhi territory showed her with "strikingly Yuezhi facial features."[650]

♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ present ♥ "The divinity of kingship seems to have been the most conspicuous element in the Kushan political system. Their kings were not only accorded the title of 'devaputra' (Son of God), corresponding to the Chinese imperial title "t'ien-tzu" (Son of Heaven), but were deified after death and their statues were set up in a devakula (god house)." [651]

"The Kushan kings derived their royal power from divine patrons, and so they were charismatic kings, human incarnations of divine might and power. As a consequence of their charisma, they also became objects of divine worship in dynastic sanctuaries." [652]

♠ Automatic deification ♣ inferred present ♥ Implied from the existence of "dynastic sanctuaries", though rulers were already gods in life. "The Kushan kings derived their royal power from divine patrons, and so they were charismatic kings, human incarnations of divine might and power. As a consequence of their charisma, they also became objects of divine worship in dynastic sanctuaries." [653]

♠ Rulers are gods ♣ present ♥ "The Kushan kings derived their royal power from divine patrons, and so they were charismatic kings, human incarnations of divine might and power. As a consequence of their charisma, they also became objects of divine worship in dynastic sanctuaries." [654]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ absent ♥ "The Kushan kings derived their royal power from divine patrons, and so they were charismatic kings, human incarnations of divine might and power. As a consequence of their charisma, they also became objects of divine worship in dynastic sanctuaries." [656] Moreover, slavery was common [657].

♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ absent ♥ "The Kushan kings derived their royal power from divine patrons, and so they were charismatic kings, human incarnations of divine might and power. As a consequence of their charisma, they also became objects of divine worship in dynastic sanctuaries." [658]
♠ Ideological thought equates elites and commoners ♣ inferred absent ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [659], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Though little can be reconstructed about the latter, Greek religion, Hinduism, Zoroastrianism and Buddhism all make a clear distinction between elites and commoners. In Greek religion and philosophy, only mystery were truly egalitarian [660]; Hinduism is well-known from its caste system [661]; Zoroastrians believed that elites were cosmologically distinct from commoners ('The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[662]). Indeed, this fits well with what little is known about Kushan social structure" "There is some direct, and a great deal of indirect, evidence to show that the commune occupied an important place in the socio-economic life of Central Asia and in the ancient East as a whole. This seems to have continued until the Early Middle Ages, for which evidence is available. Thus, the commune in Sogdiana was known as naf; it consisted of the aristocracy (aza ̄t, azatkar), merchants (xvakar), and free peasants (who were members of the commune) and craftsmen (karikar). Of these three categories in the naf the highest status was enjoyed by the azat that is, persons of ‘high and noble birth’, the azatkar or free persons associated with the azat and the ‘children of the azat of aristocratic, noble origin’." [663]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of inequality ♣ present ♥ "The Kushan kings derived their royal power from divine patrons, and so they were charismatic kings, human incarnations of divine might and power. ♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ [absent; present] ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [672], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Little is known about the latter. However, it is known that, of the other four "religions", only Greek thought promoted monogamy [673][674][675][676].

♠ Ideology reinforces prosociality ♣ inferred present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [677], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. That prosociality was an important moral value in the Kushan empire may be inferred, perhaps, from the fact that it is promoted by all four of the above-mentioned world religions (not much is known about the Indo-Iranian cults, unfortunately). Buddhism: “The twofold benefit of living a morally good life is linked to a twofold motivation: ‘Protecting oneself, one protects others; protecting others, one protects oneself ’ - just as each acrobat in a balancing act protects his partner by concentrating on himself, and protects himself by concentrating on his partner (see SN 47:19). If we take care of our own spiritual development, we render a service to others; and if we develop love towards others, we thereby also help ourselves. Accordingly, it is explicitly stated, someone who pursues the path of salvation only for his or her own benefit is to be censured, while the one who follows the path for one’s own benefit and for the benefit of others is to be commended (see AN 7:64).” [678] Hinduism: “According to Hindu Dharmashastras, of the four stages of life, the person who is at the householder stage is the anchor of society. The householder’s business is the maintenance and support of those in the other three stages of life: the students who have not yet entered into the world of work, the retirees who have seen the birth of grand- children and spotted their first gray hairs, and the renunciants, those who have left behind not only the world of work and material possession but who have also left behind family and self-interest for a life focused on spiritual freedom. Householders are the ones with material wealth, and their responsibility, their dharma, is to share it with others. [...] The link between the world of the householder and the world of the renunciant is gift giving—dana. The classic image is of the renouncer with the begging bowl, making the rounds for alms. On a daily basis, in Hindu society, food is given to sannyasins and sadhus, the world renouncers who have “cast off” from the settled world of “this shore.” ” [679] “The philosophers taught various versions of the Golden Rule, whereas traditional Greek morality said it was best to help one’s friends and harm one’s enemies. The only philosopher I can think of who specifically advocates “helping people” over “living luxuriously” is the Stoic Musonius Rufus from the first century CE, but he may have been an innovator in that respect. In general the Greeks had no religious or philosophical teachings to compare with Jewish and Christian teachings about almsgiving, gleaning, or caring for “widows and orphans.” Greek cities sometimes gave stipends to orphans if their fathers had died in battle defending the city. The most important traditional religious teaching on this subject was that the gods required people to treat “strangers and suppliants” well. That is, you should assist strangers who come to your door in need (and definitely not harm them). You can see this when Odysseus disguised as a beggar receives hospitality (Homer was a basic school text in the Hellenistic period) or in the Hellenistic myth of Baucis and Philemon, a very poor elderly couple who received two strangers and gave them hospitality. The strangers turned out to be Zeus and Hermes, who rewarded the couple. The belief that the gods “tested” humans by coming down to earth was common Hellenistic Asia Minor, where Paul and Barnabas were mistaken for Zeus and Hermes in disguise (Acts 14). Hellenistic philanthropy was closely tied to piety because the benefactions were usually things like sponsoring a festival or enhancing a sanctuary. Sponsoring a festival meant entertainment and free food distribution, but the main goal was not necessarily to help the poor—it was more to enhance the public good. I think it is very likely that the teachings of philosophers encouraged these sorts of activities, but of course the donors also benefited from increased prestige.” [680] Zoroastrianism: Cantera says that 'from its very beginnings Zoroastrianism has developed an ethical imperative of assistance to the needy members of the community'.[681]

♠ charity ♣ inferred present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [682], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. That charity was an important moral value in the Kushan empire may be inferred, perhaps, from the fact that it is promoted by all four of the above-mentioned world religions (not much is known about the Indo-Iranian cults, unfortunately). Buddhism: “Living a morally good life thus always brings a twofold benefit: Others are aided and one’s own spiritual development or karmic disposition is positively affected. This point is captured in one of the central Buddhist virtues: dana, which is ‘giving’ or ‘generosity’. ‘Giving’ creates a win-win situation. Not only is the gift’s recipient benefited but also the gift’s donor - particularly from a Buddhist point of view, since through giving one counters attachment in its deep-seated form of greedy grasping, of not letting-go.” [683] Hinduism: “According to Hindu Dharmashastras, of the four stages of life, the person who is at the householder stage is the anchor of society. The householder’s business is the maintenance and support of those in the other three stages of life: the students who have not yet entered into the world of work, the retirees who have seen the birth of grand- children and spotted their first gray hairs, and the renunciants, those who have left behind not only the world of work and material possession but who have also left behind family and self-interest for a life focused on spiritual freedom. Householders are the ones with material wealth, and their responsibility, their dharma, is to share it with others. [...] The link between the world of the householder and the world of the renunciant is gift giving—dana. The classic image is of the renouncer with the begging bowl, making the rounds for alms. On a daily basis, in Hindu society, food is given to sannyasins and sadhus, the world renouncers who have “cast off” from the settled world of “this shore.” ” [684] “The philosophers taught various versions of the Golden Rule, whereas traditional Greek morality said it was best to help one’s friends and harm one’s enemies. The only philosopher I can think of who specifically advocates “helping people” over “living luxuriously” is the Stoic Musonius Rufus from the first century CE, but he may have been an innovator in that respect. In general the Greeks had no religious or philosophical teachings to compare with Jewish and Christian teachings about almsgiving, gleaning, or caring for “widows and orphans.” Greek cities sometimes gave stipends to orphans if their fathers had died in battle defending the city. The most important traditional religious teaching on this subject was that the gods required people to treat “strangers and suppliants” well. That is, you should assist strangers who come to your door in need (and definitely not harm them). You can see this when Odysseus disguised as a beggar receives hospitality (Homer was a basic school text in the Hellenistic period) or in the Hellenistic myth of Baucis and Philemon, a very poor elderly couple who received two strangers and gave them hospitality. The strangers turned out to be Zeus and Hermes, who rewarded the couple. The belief that the gods “tested” humans by coming down to earth was common Hellenistic Asia Minor, where Paul and Barnabas were mistaken for Zeus and Hermes in disguise (Acts 14). Hellenistic philanthropy was closely tied to piety because the benefactions were usually things like sponsoring a festival or enhancing a sanctuary. Sponsoring a festival meant entertainment and free food distribution, but the main goal was not necessarily to help the poor—it was more to enhance the public good. I think it is very likely that the teachings of philosophers encouraged these sorts of activities, but of course the donors also benefited from increased prestige.” [685] Zoroastrianism: Cantera says that 'from its very beginnings Zoroastrianism has developed an ethical imperative of assistance to the needy members of the community'.[686]
♠ production of public goods ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ communal dining ♣ inferred present ♥ Buddhist panal art depicts feasting, although they also might depict scenes from a drama.[687] "In Buddhist monasteries at Shaikhan Dheri, a site in the Gandharan region, archeologists have found many wine storage jars and distillation equipment. It is a puzzling find, suggesting that Buddhist monasteries were producing liquor, even though such activities were contrary to their centuries-old monastic discipline."[688]
♠ morality ♣ inferred present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [689], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Little is known about the latter, but Buddhism, Hinduism, Greek thought and Zoroastrianism all contain claims regarding proper moral behaviour. Buddhism: “If ‘thirst’ and ‘attachment’ constitute the one root of suffering - both the suffering one undergoes oneself in sansaric existence, and the suffering one inflicts upon others - it follows that any successful attempt to reduce one’s ‘thirst’ and to moderate one’s ‘attachment’ will also result in a life causing less suffering (at least less deliberately-inflicted suffering) to others - and this is what morality basically means for Buddhism: reducing suffering, alleviating suffering, causing less suffering. Morality is an intrinsic part of the path to salvation because it is part and parcel of the salvific process of overcoming ‘thirst’ and ‘attachment’.” [690] Hinduism: “Universal dharma relates to all humanity and it includes ethical virtues such as compassion, generosity, gratitude, and non-violence. These virtues that are said to be common to all humans are known as sadhārana (common) or sanatana (eternal) dharma. For example, the Law of Manu (X:63) argues that non-injury, truth, not stealing, purity and control of the senses summarise dharma for the four castes. Elsewhere Manu (VI:91- 2) asserts that avoiding backbiting, hypocrisy, greed, infatuation, anger and discontent is approved dharma for all the stages of life. The problem is that there are different views as to what the actuality of universal dharma is. Radhakrishnan, for example, argues for a sanatana dharma, based upon Advaita Vedanta thought, that is said to be present in all religious traditions. It is a kind of perennial philosophy that is at the heart of every religious tradition not just at the heart of the Hindu tradition. Others concentrate upon a universal dharma within the Hindu tradition itself but there is no absolute consensus as to what it is. Nevertheless, in theory, the ethical virtues of compassion, generosity, gratitude, and non-violence are universally regarded by most Hindus.” [691] Greek thought: “The philosophers taught various versions of the Golden Rule, whereas traditional Greek morality said it was best to help one’s friends and harm one’s enemies. The only philosopher I can think of who specifically advocates “helping people” over “living luxuriously” is the Stoic Musonius Rufus from the first century CE, but he may have been an innovator in that respect. In general the Greeks had no religious or philosophical teachings to compare with Jewish and Christian teachings about almsgiving, gleaning, or caring for “widows and orphans.” Greek cities sometimes gave stipends to orphans if their fathers had died in battle defending the city. The most important traditional religious teaching on this subject was that the gods required people to treat “strangers and suppliants” well. That is, you should assist strangers who come to your door in need (and definitely not harm them). You can see this when Odysseus disguised as a beggar receives hospitality (Homer was a basic school text in the Hellenistic period) or in the Hellenistic myth of Baucis and Philemon, a very poor elderly couple who received two strangers and gave them hospitality. The strangers turned out to be Zeus and Hermes, who rewarded the couple. The belief that the gods “tested” humans by coming down to earth was common Hellenistic Asia Minor, where Paul and Barnabas were mistaken for Zeus and Hermes in disguise (Acts 14). Hellenistic philanthropy was closely tied to piety because the benefactions were usually things like sponsoring a festival or enhancing a sanctuary. Sponsoring a festival meant entertainment and free food distribution, but the main goal was not necessarily to help the poor—it was more to enhance the public good. I think it is very likely that the teachings of philosophers encouraged these sorts of activities, but of course the donors also benefited from increased prestige.” [692] Zoroastrianism: for a Zoroastrian worshipper, "The moral requirements of his faith make him self-reliant, bearing the responsibility for his own conduct and ultimate fate". [693]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Slavery[edit]

♠ Ideology forbids slavery for co-ethnics ♣ inferred absent ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [694], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Though little is known about the latter, neither Buddhism [695], Hinduism [696] nor Greek thought [697] condemned slavery at the time. As for Zoroastrianism, it is difficult to find direct evidence of Achaemenid-period Zoroastrian attitudes to slavery, but 'there can be no doubt that the various departments of the Achaemenid court included a large number of slaves'.[698], and 'Under the Achaemenids in Babylonia and other conquered countries Persian nobles became large slave owners'.[699] If there was religious doctrine forbidding ownership of slaves, it was evidently not observed by the Achaemenid Persian elite. In later Sasanian law, founded in Zoroastrian principles, we find a distinction between freeborn men and slaves, which is inconsistent with a doctrinal rejection of the institution of slavery.[700] Finally, it is worth noting that slave labour was an important component of the Kushan economy [701].
♠ Ideology forbids slavery for co-religionists ♣ inferred absent ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [702], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Though little is known about the latterm neither Buddhism [703], Hinduism [704] nor Greek thought [705] condemned slavery at the time. As for Zoroastrianism, it is difficult to find direct evidence of Achaemenid-period Zoroastrian attitudes to slavery, but 'there can be no doubt that the various departments of the Achaemenid court included a large number of slaves'.[706], and 'Under the Achaemenids in Babylonia and other conquered countries Persian nobles became large slave owners'.[707] If there was religious doctrine forbidding ownership of slaves, it was evidently not observed by the Achaemenid Persian elite. In later Sasanian law, founded in Zoroastrian principles, we find a distinction between freeborn men and slaves, which is inconsistent with a doctrinal rejection of the institution of slavery.[708] Finally, it is worth noting that slave labour was an important component of the Kushan economy [709].
♠ Ideology forbids slavery universally ♣ inferred absent ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [710], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. In later Sasanian law, founded in Zoroastrian principles, we find a distinction between freeborn men and slaves, which is inconsistent with a doctrinal rejection of the institution of slavery.[716] Finally, it is worth noting that slave labour was an important component of the Kushan economy [717].

Normative Ideological Aspects of Human Sacrifice[edit]

♠ Human sacrifice ♣ [0-1] ♥ Inferred absent. At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [718], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. That human sacrifice was not practiced in the Kushan empire may perhaps be inferred from the fact it was not commonly practiced in either in Greek religion, Hinduism, Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism. Buddhism categorically condemns the act of killing [719]. Greeks "talked and mythologized a lot about human sacrifice [...] but they don’t seem to have practiced it during the historical period.” [720] As for Zoroastrianism, there appears to be only one reference to human sacrifice among the Achaemenids (the best-known Zoroastrian polity among those preceding the Kushan empire), and it is an unreliable one [721]. And as for Hinduism, human sacrifice seems to be mostly associated with Vedic times and with nineteenth-century Goddess worship [722]. However, it is entirely possible that human sacrifice was practiced as part of some or all of the Indo-Iranian cults that the Kushan elite patronised alongside these four religions, but not enough seems to be known about them to know for sure.

Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

Supernatural beings (e.g., gods, spirits, ancestors)

♠ Number of officially sanctioned supernatural beings ♣ polytheistic ♥
♠ Omnipresent supernatural beings ♣ [present; absent] ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [771], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "Greeks did not necessarily assume that the gods could read their minds and knew the inner secrets of their heart and so forth. However, they definitely assumed that gods could see and hear everything they did. I don’t think the gods were concerned with people’s thoughts unless these thoughts manifested themselves in words or actions. This is not to say that they could never read minds, just that it wasn’t part and parcel of how they were represented, the way it is, for example, with the Christian god.” [772] In Hinduism: "Each Purana tends to focus on a specific deity as the Supreme Being responsible for creating and ordering the world. [...] Thus, [for example,] Krishna [...] is a form of the supreme God who creates, maintains, and destroys the universe. In the Bhagavad Gita , Arjuna is permitted to glimpse Krishna’s cosmic form. He sees the entire universe, the sun, stars, and planets, and all the people gathered on the battlefield within the body of Krishna. Krishna tells him that everything that exists is part of himself, but that he is not limited to everything that exists. In other words, just as the Vedic Purusha extended ten finger-lengths beyond the created universe, so too Krishna is the entire cosmos and more." [773] In Zoroastrianism: "The name of the supreme God of the Zoroastrian faith is Ahura Mazda (or Ohrmazd). He is self-created, omniscient, omnipresent, holy, invisible, and beyond human conceptualization (Yasna 4.7, 29.4, 31.13, 45.5; Yasht 1.7, 1.8, 1.12, 1.13-14; Videvdat 2.1, 19.20)."[774] In Buddhism: "There are numerous Higher Gods, but these are remote and sublime beings who have little involvement in human affairs. Nevertheless, they are subject to karma and are eventually reborn like everyone else." [775]
♠ Omniscient supernatural beings ♣ [present; absent] ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [776], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not enough appears to be known about the latter to be able to say whether the gods they focussed on were thought to be omniscient. And, similarly, it is not very clear whether omniscience would have been seen as a characteristic of Siva. However, even by looking only at the remaining three religions, it is clear that this variable cannot be coded as either "absent" or "present". Regarding Buddhism, the idea of an all-knowing God runs contrary to the emphasis that this religion places on an individual's responsibility for their own spiritual development [777]. Regarding Zoroastrianism, the god Ahura-Mazda is a fairly straightforward omniscient deity [778]. And as for Greek deities, “Everything depends on what you mean by “omniscience.” First, there is the distinction between “knowing everything important,” like who committed a crime or who will win the war, and true omniscience, which includes everything which can be known (things like “the fall of a sparrow” and how many grains of sand are on a beach). Very few Greek gods claimed true omniscience. The only example I know is that it was claimed for Apollo at Delphi, who was specifically an oracular god. So it was his business to know everything. The claim seems to have been put forward by the sanctuary in order to enhance his prestige. Greek philosophers presumably believed in divine omniscience because they represented the gods or the divine as perfect, which would require absolute knowledge, but these issues do not seem to have really come to the fore in the literature until the Roman period and Christianity, so far as I can tell. Even if omniscience only means “knowing everything that it is important to know,” I have my doubts about whether the Greek gods possessed it. I would say that it was always contextual. They knew whatever it was important to know in a given context. Greeks did not necessarily assume that the gods could read their minds and knew the inner secrets of their heart and so forth. However, they definitely assumed that gods could see and hear everything they did. I don’t think the gods were concerned with people’s thoughts unless these thoughts manifested themselves in words or actions. This is not to say that they could never read minds, just that it wasn’t part and parcel of how they were represented, the way it is, for example, with the Christian god.” [779]
♠ Omnipotent supernatural beings ♣ [present; absent] ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [780], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: In Zoroastrianism: "In taking the immense step of seeing Ahura Mazda as God, rather than only as one of the great gods of the Iranian pantheon, Zoroaster stopped short of conceiving him as omnipotent. According to his revelation, suffering and evil in this world, and torment in the next, have a source other than Ahura Mazda. He can diminish and in the end annihilate them, but not control or direct them in the present."[781] In Buddhism: "There are numerous Higher Gods, but these are remote and sublime beings who have little involvement in human affairs. Nevertheless, they are subject to karma and are eventually reborn like everyone else." [782]
♠ High Gods (creator gods) ♣ [moralizing; inactive] ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [783], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: In Hinduism: "Among the cosmic laws that constitute dharma, the law of karma, or action, is especially relevant to rebirth. By the law of karma, every thought, word, and deed carries with it a like reaction. Beings are born, live, die, and are reborn based on their actions. Every good deed brings a reward, and every bad deed a punishment. The terms ‘reward’ and ‘punishment’ are deceptive, however; for karma is not seen as a set of rewards and punishments doled out by a just God, but in impersonal terms, as the working of natural law. Karma attracts reactions to moral agents like an ethical law of gravity. Benevolent actions produce good results for the person who engages in them. Similarly, actions intended to cause suffering will lead to suffering for those who commit them." [784] In Zoroastrianism, the following quote makes it clear the supreme god, Ahura Mazda, was both responsible for the creation of the world and for punishing transgressors. "[T]he prophet feels also the awe due to the God who is “Creator of all” (Y. 44.7), and who, as Lord of aša, presides over a strict justice for mankind by which the wicked, who have chosen to act “at Lie’s commands” (Y. 31.1) will be in due course condemned. Their destination is the Worst Existence, i.e., Hell, which has been brought into being by Angra Mainyu (Y. 30.4). Mazdā has “appointed recompenses” (Y. 43.5), decreeing that “the end shall be different for each” (Y. 48.4); but he “has left to men’s wills (the choice between) holy and unholy” (Y. 45.9), and it is their own inner selves (daēnā-) which lead sinners to destruction (Y. 51.13)."[785] In Buddhism: "There are numerous Higher Gods, but these are remote and sublime beings who have little involvement in human affairs. Nevertheless, they are subject to karma and are eventually reborn like everyone else." [786]

Supernatural Enforcement of Morality

For this section, include any type of supernatural punishment or reward either in this lifetime (e.g., curses, good fortune) or in the afterlife (e.g., hell, reincarnation). This includes generalized impersonal punishment/reward (e.g., karma, mana) as well as punishment by specific supernatural beings. Include specific details of the nature and extent of punishment/reward in the description. Code “compound” moral actions (e.g., filial piety = kin + respect) in multiple categories as necessary. Morality categories are based on Moral Foundations Theory (Haidt & Graham, 2007) and Morality-As-Cooperation theory (Curry, 2016).
♠ supernatural enforcement against harm ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [787], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "The most important traditional religious teaching on this subject was that the gods required people to treat “strangers and suppliants” well. That is, you should assist strangers who come to your door in need (and definitely not harm them). You can see this when Odysseus disguised as a beggar receives hospitality (Homer was a basic school text in the Hellenistic period) or in the Hellenistic myth of Baucis and Philemon, a very poor elderly couple who received two strangers and gave them hospitality. The strangers turned out to be Zeus and Hermes, who rewarded the couple.” [788] In Hinduism: "Coming from the Sanskrit root word Dhar (sustain), Dharma has been interpreted variously as a religious code of behavior, a divine system of morality and righteousness, and conformation to one’s cosmically allotted duty and nature (Radhakrishnan, 1968). It is a comprehensive term, incorporating benevolence to humanity, moral restraint (yama), and observance of duty or obligation (niyama). Yama required the active avoidance of violence (ahimsa), dishonesty, falsehood, jealousy, avarice, and other vices." [789] Hinduism: "Coming from the Sanskrit root word Dhar (sustain), Dharma has been interpreted variously as a religious code of behavior, a divine system of morality and righteousness, and conformation to one’s cosmically allotted duty and nature (Radhakrishnan, 1968). It is a comprehensive term, incorporating benevolence to humanity, moral restraint (yama), and observance of duty or obligation (niyama). Yama required the active avoidance of violence (ahimsa), dishonesty, falsehood, jealousy, avarice, and other vices." [790] In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians. The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[791] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include homicide, "neglecting crying and hungry children" and "denying hospitality to travellers"[792]. In Buddhism: "The heart of Buddhist spiritual life, whether lay or monastic, is punya (Pali punna ). This term is usually translated as ‘merit’ and refers to certain significant kinds of action that are regarded as auspicious and potent deeds. Essentially, merit is good ‘karma’ (karman/kamma) : wholesome (kusla/kusala)deeds which plant seeds that bear fortunate and pleasant fruits, whether in this life or lives to come, and thereby create conditions for further meritorious action in the future. These are the kinds of action that conduce to the development of the qualities of the eightfold path. Moreover it is making merit that binds the lay and monastic communities together. The traditional ‘bases of merit’ are generosity (dana), ethical conduct (sila/sila ), and meditation (bhavana ). [...]The practice of good conduct takes the form of commitment to the five precepts usually recited in conjunction with the three refuges" which include "to refrain from harming living creatures"."[793] "The nature of bad action is usually illustrated by reference to a list of the ten courses of unwholesome action (karma/kamma-patha)which consist of three bodily courses of action (taking life, taking what is not given, sexual misconduct), four vocal courses of action (lying, divisive speech, hurtful speech, frivolous speech), and three mental courses of action (covetousness, ill-will, wrong view). [...] Essentially the psychological states that motivate the ten unwholesome courses of action - strong greed, hatred, and delusion - lead to rebirth in the unhappy destinies or ‘descents’: in a hell realm, as a hungry ghost, an animal, or a jealous god. In fact rather a precise correlation exists here: dominated by greed one becomes a hungry ghost, a class of beings ever discontent and anguished because of being unable to satisfy their greed; dominated by hatred one enters one of the hell realms where one suffers terrible pain; dominated by ignorance one becomes an animal ruled by the instincts of food and reproduction." [794]
♠ supernatural enforcement of fairness ♣ [present; absent] ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [795], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: Food was distributed during certain sacrificial ceremonies, but it was done to gain prestige rather than for any reward from the gods[796]. In Hinduism: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [797] "Coming from the Sanskrit root word Dhar (sustain), Dharma has been interpreted variously as a religious code of behavior, a divine system of morality and righteousness, and conformation to one’s cosmically allotted duty and nature (Radhakrishnan, 1968). It is a comprehensive term, incorporating benevolence to humanity, moral restraint (yama), and observance of duty or obligation (niyama). Yama required the active avoidance of violence (ahimsa), dishonesty, falsehood, jealousy, avarice, and other vices." [798] Hinduism: Historical Setting. In Palmer, M.D. and S.M. Burgess (eds) Wiley-Blackwell Companion to Religion and Social Justice pp. 110-123. Hoboken: John Wiley and Sons.</ref> In Zoroastrianism: "The easiest way to avoid hell, of course, is to accumulate more good thoughts, words or deeds than bad. Some virtues, however, are praised as particularly efficient to avoid hell.20 A catechism highlights gratefulness (ČHP 30 [= Kanga 1960:16-17]). This virtue is also praised in the wisdom literature as a way to save one’s soul (Dk. VI 120; E38c; E45f [= Shaked 1979:48-49; 206-7; 214-15]), sometimes in conjunction with other virtues such as contentment and tenderness. Generosity is also emphasized as saving the soul from hell (Dk. IX 6.3 [see West 1892:179]), as is righteousness (Dk. IX 17.3 [see West 1892:204])."[799] In Buddhism: "In the cosmology set out above, karma functions as the elevator that takes people from one floor of the building to another. Good deeds result in an upward movement and bad deeds in a downward one. Karma is not a system of rewards and punishments meted out by God but a kind of natural law akin to the law of gravity. [...] Buddhists speak of good karma as ‘merit’ (punna, Sanskrit: punya), and much effort is expended in acquiring it. Some picture it as a kind of spiritual capital - like money in a bank account - whereby credit is built up as the deposit on a heavenly rebirth. One of the best ways for a layman to earn merit is by supporting the order of monks. This can be done by placing food in the bowls of monks as they pass on their daily alms round, by providing robes for the monks, by listening to sermons and attending religious services, and by donating funds for the upkeep of monasteries and temples." [800] "The nature of bad action is usually illustrated by reference to a list of the ten courses of unwholesome action (karma/kamma-patha)which consist of three bodily courses of action (taking life, taking what is not given, sexual misconduct), four vocal courses of action (lying, divisive speech, hurtful speech, frivolous speech), and three mental courses of action (covetousness, ill-will, wrong view). [...] Essentially the psychological states that motivate the ten unwholesome courses of action - strong greed, hatred, and delusion - lead to rebirth in the unhappy destinies or ‘descents’: in a hell realm, as a hungry ghost, an animal, or a jealous god. In fact rather a precise correlation exists here: dominated by greed one becomes a hungry ghost, a class of beings ever discontent and anguished because of being unable to satisfy their greed; dominated by hatred one enters one of the hell realms where one suffers terrible pain; dominated by ignorance one becomes an animal ruled by the instincts of food and reproduction." [801]
♠ supernatural enforcement of human reciprocity ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [802], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "It is now evident that, for Hesiod, κακότης‎ represents moral badness and the opposite to virtue, arete. A bad man (κακὸς ἀνήρ‎) does not follow justice (δίκη‎) with his mind, and acts immorally; he represents a source of religious pollution and danger for his community. Just as Hesiod connects the bad man with injustice and religious danger, so he presents moral righteousness and adherence to justice (δίκη‎) as coexisting with ritual correctness. Gregory Nagy points out that in Op. 336-41, a passage which follows the lines quoted above, the moral correctness of δίκη‎ is ‘programmatically re-enforced by the ritual correctness of the just man’:26 Perses is again reminded that gods punish unjust deeds (ἄδικα ἔργα‎; vv. 325-34), and that he should often sacrifice purely and cleanly to the deathless gods and propitiate them often with libations and incense (vv. 336-41). Religious correctness is thus laid out as avoiding unjust deeds and as honouring the gods with sacrifices and libations.//"In vv. 325-34 the following deeds are listed as unjust (ἄδικα ἔργα‎): gaining wealth by force, stealing, or deception; wronging a suppliant or a guest; engaging in illicit sexual intercourse; offending orphans or aged parents. These offences span both the social and religious spheres. This is stressed at the end of the passage, where Hesiod states that such deeds provoke the anger of Zeus himself, who inflicts grave punishments (vv. 333-4). Effectively, Zeus holds the transgressor in agos. To sum up, in Hesiod’s poem, justice is represented as a goddess and as a law given to humans by Zeus. It governs all social and religious relationships; it encompasses religious correctness. Moral goodness equals adherence to justice, whereas ‘badness’ (κακία‎ or κακότης‎) is equal to injustice and religious transgression."[803] "The most important traditional religious teaching on this subject was that the gods required people to treat “strangers and suppliants” well. That is, you should assist strangers who come to your door in need (and definitely not harm them). You can see this when Odysseus disguised as a beggar receives hospitality (Homer was a basic school text in the Hellenistic period) or in the Hellenistic myth of Baucis and Philemon, a very poor elderly couple who received two strangers and gave them hospitality. The strangers turned out to be Zeus and Hermes, who rewarded the couple.” [804] In Hinduism: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [805] In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians. The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[806] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include "violation of contracts", "selling items with false measures and weights" and "making false promises"[807] In Buddhism: "The nature of bad action is usually illustrated by reference to a list of the ten courses of unwholesome action (karma/kamma-patha)which consist of three bodily courses of action (taking life, taking what is not given, sexual misconduct), four vocal courses of action (lying, divisive speech, hurtful speech, frivolous speech), and three mental courses of action (covetousness, ill-will, wrong view). [...] Essentially the psychological states that motivate the ten unwholesome courses of action - strong greed, hatred, and delusion - lead to rebirth in the unhappy destinies or ‘descents’: in a hell realm, as a hungry ghost, an animal, or a jealous god. In fact rather a precise correlation exists here: dominated by greed one becomes a hungry ghost, a class of beings ever discontent and anguished because of being unable to satisfy their greed; dominated by hatred one enters one of the hell realms where one suffers terrible pain; dominated by ignorance one becomes an animal ruled by the instincts of food and reproduction." [808]

[811] "Universal dharma relates to all humanity and it includes ethical virtues such as compassion, generosity, gratitude, and non-violence. These virtues that are said to be common to all humans are known as sadharana (common) or sanatana (eternal) dharma. For example, the Law of Manu (X:63) argues that non-injury, truth, not stealing, purity and control of the senses summarise dharma for the four castes." ♠ supernatural enforcement of kinship obligations ♣ suspected unknown ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [817], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "Although Homer's epic depicts a few exceptional figures like Tantalos and Sisyphos receiving punishment in the underworld (Od. 11 576-600), elaborate descriptions of underworld retributions come mostly from later sources, causing some scholars to suppose that a compensatory afterlife is a later invention. However, not only does such compensation appear in our earliest textual source, but Pausanias (10.28.4-6) tells us of the great painting of the underworld by the fifth-century BCE painter Polygnotos, which included such depictions. [...] A man who treated his father unjustly is being throttled by him, while another who plundered a temple is being tormented by a female skilled in poisons. Pausanias comments that the Greeks in Polygnotos' time thought that failing to respect parents and the gods were the worst crimes, and that is why Polygnotos has illustrated these cases. Polygnotos' painting, while exceptional in size and scope, is not exceptional in its subject matter: numerous vase paintings show scenes of underworld reward or punishment, and a Demosthenic speech mentions paintings depicting the afterlife torments of the impious as a familiar trope for his audience ([Dem.] 25.53)."[818] In Hinduism: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [819] "As a religiously informed orientation to life’s actions and obligations, dharma gives guidelines to how husbands, wives, children, and parents should conduct themselves. Many of the selections from sources included here advise their audiences on the roles proper for various situations and consider some of the complexities and ambiguities that arise in living according to their discipline. [...] Deeply embedded in Hindu notions of marriage is a disposition toward protection. It is the husband’s obligation to protect his wife from external predators and temptations and to invest in a relationship that will sustain her commitment to him and his world. At the same time, it is the obligation of the wife to protect her husband by feeding, caring for him, and investing in a relationship that will sustain his commitment." [820] In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians. The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[821] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include "disrespect of husband" and "disrespect for one's parents"[822]. In Buddhism: "The heart of Buddhist spiritual life, whether lay or monastic, is punya (Pali punna ). [...]"[823] "The nature of bad action is usually illustrated by reference to a list of the ten courses of unwholesome action (karma/kamma-patha)which consist of three bodily courses of action (taking life, taking what is not given, sexual misconduct), four vocal courses of action (lying, divisive speech, hurtful speech, frivolous speech), and three mental courses of action (covetousness, ill-will, wrong view). [824] "‘Right action’ basically means to act gently and kindly and to be ‘compassionate to all living beings’. What ‘right action’ and ‘right livelihood’ positively involve becomes clearer in the numerous practical guidelines for dealing with one’s parents, teachers, spouse, children, friends, one’s master and servant, Brahmans and Sramanas. So, for example, in the very popular Sigalovada Sutta (DN 31): Children should honour their parents, be grateful and support them when they are in need or grow old; parents should show their love to their children by teaching them virtue, training them for a profession and passing on their inheritance when it is due; [...] a husband should minister to his wife ‘by honouring her, by not disparaging her, by not being unfaithful to her, by giving authority to her, by providing her with adornments’. A wife will reciprocate ‘by properly organising her work, by being kind to the servants, by not being unfaithful, by protecting stores, and by being skilful and diligent in all she has to do’." [825]
♠ supernatural enforcement of in-group loyalty ♣ inferred absent ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [826], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians. The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[827] Staufield's comprehensive list of all the sins mentioned in the Arda Viraz does not include anything that might be linked to in-group loyalty. In Buddhism: "As mentioned before, the development of a loving mind towards all beings, whether friend or foe, is the aim of one of the meditative practices of early Buddhism (see above p. 53f ). In his instructions for this kind of meditation, Buddhaghosa recommends starting with a wish for one’s own happiness: ‘May I be happy and free from suffering.’ Only if one is able to love oneself will one be capable of extending the same love to others. And so Buddhaghosa then suggests directing this loving mental attitude to a dear friend, then to a neutral person and finally towards a hostile person until it becomes possible to bear love towards all beings indiscriminately, such that one sincerely wishes that ‘all may be happy and free from suffering’ (see Vism 9:1- 12). Buddhaghosa is realistic enough to expect some difficulties: If someone is unable to overcome ill feelings against a particular person, one should imagine that in a former reincarnation the person in question has been one’s own beloved mother, father, brother, sister, son, daughter or friend. For, as Buddhaghosa quotes the canonical scriptures, within the unfathomable length of saņsaric existence ‘it is not easy to find a being who has not formerly been your mother … your father …’ (SN 15:14- 19), etc. (see Vism 9:36; Nanamoli, 1999, p. 297f ). Making oneself aware of the universal kinship of all beings should aid in becoming free of negative feelings. The crucial importance of overcoming every form of hatred or ill will was emphasised by the Buddha himself in his parable of the saw, which Buddhaghosa recalls: Even if bandits would cut one literally to pieces with a sharp saw, one should not develop any hatred or hostility against them, but encompass them with loving-kindness (see MN 21)." [828] "[...] Buddhism maintained a fairly flexible posture vis-a-vis competing religions and never sought to require laity to identify themselves exclusively as Buddhist or to rely singularly on the Buddhist clergy for all their spiritual needs." [829]
♠ supernatural enforcement of respect ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [830], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults.
