[831] "The Brahmins, although ‘priests’, were higher in the caste hierarchy than the Kshatriyas, the ‘rulers’. Not all Brahmins have been priests and not all Kshatriyas have been rulers. The complexity of the caste system, regional and linguistic differences and historical change have all militated against this rigidity. [...]Brahmins have traditionally been seen as ritually more pure than Kshatriyas and more versed in religious knowledge. They are, in theory if not always in fact, the key scholars in the Hindu tradition. The Kshatriyas have traditionally been seen, again in theory if not always in practice, as the rulers who maintain society, uphold dharma and protect the householder system. They built and sponsored temples, protected their subjects and their deities and, to that extent, the Brahmins recognised a ruler’s overlordship. However, the kingly role in ruling and guardianship duties involved more ‘polluting tendencies’ than were usual in a Brahmin’s life. The Brahmins have had a dual role in society. Those who are active priests in temples have engaged in functions that involve being given gifts by worshippers and such temple priests are more involved with lower castes. Paradoxically Brahmins who are versed in promoting scholarship and in advancing religious learning but who do not act as serving priests are, in general, more widely admired because they have less contact with the public. Thus Brahmins who are not priests can be more akin to those renouncers engaged in lifelong learning and spirituality, rather than in the hurly-burly of householder life. To that extent they would receive greater respect than Kshatriyas." [832] In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians. The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[833] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include "disrespect of husband"[834]. In Buddhism: "In the cosmology set out above, karma functions as the elevator that takes people from one floor of the building to another. Good deeds result in an upward movement and bad deeds in a downward one. Karma is not a system of rewards and punishments meted out by God but a kind of natural law akin to the law of gravity. [...] Buddhists speak of good karma as ‘merit’ (punna, Sanskrit: punya), and much effort is expended in acquiring it. Some picture it as a kind of spiritual capital - like money in a bank account - whereby credit is built up as the deposit on a heavenly rebirth. One of the best ways for a layman to earn merit is by supporting the order of monks. This can be done by placing food in the bowls of monks as they pass on their daily alms round, by providing robes for the monks, by listening to sermons and attending religious services, and by donating funds for the upkeep of monasteries and temples." [835]
♠ supernatural enforcement of bravery ♣ [absent; present] ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [836], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: Though andreia (manliness/bravery) was a very important cultural value, rewards for it and punishments for its opposite came from one's peers, not the gods[837]. In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians. The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[838] Staufield's comprehensive list of all the sins mentioned in the Arda Viraz does not include anything that might be linked to bravery. In Buddhism: "In marked contrast to the view of Islam about the fate of warriors who die in a holy war, the Buddha expresses the view in the Sutta Nipata (Sn.iv.308-11) that soldiers who die in battle go not to a special paradise but to a special hell, since at the moment of death their minds were bent on killing. Some texts affirm that even killing in self-defence, or in defence of family or friends, is wrongful, and in general an attitude of non-resistance in the face of violence is commended. The commentary to the Dhammapada relates how on one occasion when the Buddha's kinsmen, the Sakyas, were under attack, they allowed themselves to be slaughtered rather than break the First Precept. Other examples in the Jatakas and elsewhere speak of princes and kings who renounced their thrones rather than resorting to violence to defend their kingdoms." [839] However, in Hinduism: "Early Jainism was prepared to countenance the exercise of martial skills in war but only if carried out defensively, and it denied the possibility, frequently stated in Hinduism, that a warrior who died in battle would be reborn in heaven (Bh 7.9)."[840]
♠ supernatural enforcement of purity ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [841], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "Hesiod is generally quick to remark that the gods are pleased when the rites are performed correctly, and are displeased when proper ritual purity is not obtained. [...] We can also observe different grades of divine anger: libating with unwashed hands renders the ritual null and void, and the gods refuse to communicate with the worshipper. The failure to achieve the required state of purity results in the failure of the ritual. A graver and more serious offence is committed when someone crosses the river without prayer and the ritual washing—then the gods are not only irate, but also keen to punish. This makes sense: the rivers are divinities, and by entering them humans establish a particularly close contact with the divine. It is one of the basic premises of Greek religion that gods abhor pollution, which is why ritual cleansing is a necessary prerequisite for every ritual action. This would imply that entering a river requires a particularly stringent adherence to ritual purity, while avoiding this purification inspires a particularly harsh punishment—a punishment for jeopardizing the god’s own purity. The intensity of the contact with the divinity corresponds directly to the intensity of the purity required for the worshipper and to the severity of the divine punishment if this requirement is violated."[842] In Hinduism: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [843] "The strength of the caste system has been enhanced by another principle emphasised by Dumont (1980) in his classical work entitled Homo Hierarchicus: The Caste System and its Implications. According to Dumont, in addition to the distinctions already outlined that fit varnas and jatis into hierarchies, another must be added. This distinction takes caste hierarchical divides beyond the kind of class distinctions found in eighteenth-century Britain or in the Ancien Regime in Europe, by stressing the opposition between purity and pollution. Caste difference, according to this classification, goes beyond class differences into this other realm of ritual purity and pollution. The Brahmins are the highest and purest varna, and the Dalits are the lowest, in that they are outside varna and are impure altogether. The other varnas rank in between. Pollution occurs in different ways. It occurs through bodily contact of one sort or another, with menstrual cycles, through birth and death, through emissions such as faeces, urine and saliva, and through contact with night-soil, dirty clothes, unswept rooms and so on. Thus marriage and sex have to function within the correct set of jatis in the correct varna. Polluting jobs such as laundering and sweeping are done by people of lower castes so that the blood of people of higher castes can remain ‘pure’. Equally menstrual activity among older women makes them more ‘impure’ than men or pre-menstrual virgins, and therefore women are more polluting than men. They will thus often live semi-separately while menstruating, during which time they will also avoid going to the temple and therefore ‘polluting’ the gods. Furthermore, untouchable Dalits were prohibited from entering many temples before the post-Independence freedom-of-temple-entry legislation and even now they are kept out of some temples in many villages. [...] It is also true to say that Dumont’s thesis suffers from exaggeration and other matters are important in discussing caste. Nevertheless, there is at least an element of truth in the notion that purity and pollution has relevance in any discussion of caste." [844] In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians. The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[845] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include "sodomy", "sexual intercourse during menstruation" and "polluting water and fire through excrement and carrion"[846]. In Buddhism: "In marked contrast to the view of Islam about the fate of warriors who die in a holy war, the Buddha expresses the view in the Sutta Nipata (Sn.iv.308-11) that soldiers who die in battle go not to a special paradise but to a special hell, since at the moment of death their minds were bent on killing. Some texts affirm that even killing in self-defence, or in defence of family or friends, is wrongful, and in general an attitude of non-resistance in the face of violence is commended. The commentary to the Dhammapada relates how on one occasion when the Buddha's kinsmen, the Sakyas, were under attack, they allowed themselves to be slaughtered rather than break the First Precept. Other examples in the Jatakas and elsewhere speak of princes and kings who renounced their thrones rather than resorting to violence to defend their kingdoms." [847]
♠ general supernatural enforcement of morality ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [848], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "Although Homer's epic depicts a few exceptional figures like Tantalos and Sisyphos receiving punishment in the underworld (Od. 11 576-600), elaborate descriptions of underworld retributions come mostly from later sources, causing some scholars to suppose that a compensatory afterlife is a later invention. However, not only does such compensation appear in our earliest textual source, but Pausanias (10.28.4-6) tells us of the great painting of the underworld by the fifth-century BCE painter Polygnotos, which included such depictions. [...] A man who treated his father unjustly is being throttled by him, while another who plundered a temple is being tormented by a female skilled in poisons. Pausanias comments that the Greeks in Polygnotos' time thought that failing to respect parents and the gods were the worst crimes, and that is why Polygnotos has illustrated these cases. Polygnotos' painting, while exceptional in size and scope, is not exceptional in its subject matter: numerous vase paintings show scenes of underworld reward or punishment, and a Demosthenic speech mentions paintings depicting the afterlife torments of the impious as a familiar trope for his audience ([Dem.] 25.53)."[849] In Hinduism: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [850] "Coming from the Sanskrit root word Dhar (sustain), Dharma has been interpreted variously as a religious code of behavior, a divine system of morality and righteousness, and conformation to one’s cosmically allotted duty and nature (Radhakrishnan, 1968). It is a comprehensive term, incorporating benevolence to humanity, moral restraint (yama), and observance of duty or obligation (niyama). Yama required the active avoidance of violence (ahimsa), dishonesty, falsehood, jealousy, avarice, and other vices." [851] Hinduism: Historical Setting. In Palmer, M.D. and S.M. Burgess (eds) Wiley-Blackwell Companion to Religion and Social Justice pp. 110-123. Hoboken: John Wiley and Sons.</ref> In Zoroastrianism: "The easiest way to avoid hell, of course, is to accumulate more good thoughts, words or deeds than bad. Some virtues, however, are praised as particularly efficient to avoid hell.20 A catechism highlights gratefulness (ČHP 30 [= Kanga 1960:16-17]). This virtue is also praised in the wisdom literature as a way to save one’s soul (Dk. VI 120; E38c; E45f [= Shaked 1979:48-49; 206-7; 214-15]), sometimes in conjunction with other virtues such as contentment and tenderness. Generosity is also emphasized as saving the soul from hell (Dk. IX 6.3 [see West 1892:179]), as is righteousness (Dk. IX 17.3 [see West 1892:204])."[852]
♠ other supernatural enforcement of piety ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [853], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "Although Homer's epic depicts a few exceptional figures like Tantalos and Sisyphos receiving punishment in the underworld (Od. 11 576-600), elaborate descriptions of underworld retributions come mostly from later sources, causing some scholars to suppose that a compensatory afterlife is a later invention. However, not only does such compensation appear in our earliest textual source, but Pausanias (10.28.4-6) tells us of the great painting of the underworld by the fifth-century BCE painter Polygnotos, which included such depictions. [...] A man who treated his father unjustly is being throttled by him, while another who plundered a temple is being tormented by a female skilled in poisons. Pausanias comments that the Greeks in Polygnotos' time thought that failing to respect parents and the gods were the worst crimes, and that is why Polygnotos has illustrated these cases. Polygnotos' painting, while exceptional in size and scope, is not exceptional in its subject matter: numerous vase paintings show scenes of underworld reward or punishment, and a Demosthenic speech mentions paintings depicting the afterlife torments of the impious as a familiar trope for his audience ([Dem.] 25.53)."[854] In Zoroastrianism: "Likewise there are some sins that immediately lead to hell, such as performing worship while thinking that the gods do not exist (Dk. VI D1b [= Shaked 1979:176-77]), standing when urinating (MX 1.39 [Skjærvø 2005:242]), or ignorance, bad knowledge and lack of wisdom (WZ 30.38-39 [= Gignoux and Tafazzoli 1993:104-7])."[855] In Buddhism: "The heart of Buddhist spiritual life, whether lay or monastic, is punya (Pali punna ). This term is usually translated as ‘merit’ and refers to certain significant kinds of action that are regarded as auspicious and potent deeds. Essentially, merit is good ‘karma’ (karman/kamma) : wholesome (kusla/kusala)deeds which plant seeds that bear fortunate and pleasant fruits, whether in this life or lives to come, and thereby create conditions for further meritorious action in the future. These are the kinds of action that conduce to the development of the qualities of the eightfold path. Moreover it is making merit that binds the lay and monastic communities together. The traditional ‘bases of merit’ are generosity (dana), ethical conduct (sila/sila ), and meditation (bhavana ). [...]The practice of good conduct takes the form of commitment to the five precepts usually recited in conjunction with the three refuges: (1) to refrain from harming living creatures, (2) to refrain from taking what is not given, (3) to refrain from sexual misconduct, (4) to refrain from false speech, (5) to refrain from intoxicants that cause heedlessness (see pp. 170- 2). In addition the lay disciple may on special occasions (traditionally the full moon ‘observance’ days) or for longer periods take the eight precepts, replacing the third precept (refraining from sexual misconduct) with complete sexual abstinence and adding (6) refraining from eating after midday, (7) refraining from attending entertainments and using perfumes, etc., and (8) refraining from using luxurious beds."[856]
♠ other supernatural enforcement not related to piety ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [857], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: Descent into savagery/beastliness, e.g. through cannibalism. In myth, Tantalos and Lykaion are both punished for offering human flesh to the gods--which is not only a form of impiety, but also "broke the alimentary which helped articulate the cosmos; it thus endangered the cosmic order, threatening to drag the gods, who are its guarantors, to the level of bestiality to which Tantalos himself had drifted, to make the very guarantors of the cosmic order transgress one of the fundamental rules which help articulate the universe. Tantalos' transgression of the taboo against cannibalism also endangered human society, for it threatened the breakdown of civilization and a reversion to savagery, of which cannibalism was perceived to be an important characteristic."[858] In Zoroastrianism: "Likewise there are some sins that immediately lead to hell, such as performing worship while thinking that the gods do not exist (Dk. VI D1b [= Shaked 1979:176-77]), standing when urinating (MX 1.39 [Skjærvø 2005:242]), or ignorance, bad knowledge and lack of wisdom (WZ 30.38-39 [= Gignoux and Tafazzoli 1993:104-7])."[859] In Buddhism: "The heart of Buddhist spiritual life, whether lay or monastic, is punya (Pali punna ). This term is usually translated as ‘merit’ and refers to certain significant kinds of action that are regarded as auspicious and potent deeds. Essentially, merit is good ‘karma’ (karman/kamma) : wholesome (kusla/kusala)deeds which plant seeds that bear fortunate and pleasant fruits, whether in this life or lives to come, and thereby create conditions for further meritorious action in the future. These are the kinds of action that conduce to the development of the qualities of the eightfold path. Moreover it is making merit that binds the lay and monastic communities together. The traditional ‘bases of merit’ are generosity (dana), ethical conduct (sila/sila ), and meditation (bhavana ). [...]The practice of good conduct takes the form of commitment to the five precepts usually recited in conjunction with the three refuges: (1) to refrain from harming living creatures, (2) to refrain from taking what is not given, (3) to refrain from sexual misconduct, (4) to refrain from false speech, (5) to refrain from intoxicants that cause heedlessness (see pp. 170- 2). [...] ."[860]

Human Enforcement of Morality

♠ care/harm ♣ suspected unknown ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [861], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. Greek religion/ideology: a number of laws, traditions and regulations were linked to the protection of moral values[862], but many of these do not appear to be sufficiently religion-based for our interests. Hinduism: main expectation appears to have been of supernatural, not human, enforcement, specifically through reincarnation. [863] Zoroastrianism: in Sasanian Zoroastrianism, "all offenses against religious norms which could endanger the soul of the delinquent [...] were subject to punishment by religious authorities." [864] Buddhism: main expectation appears to have been of supernatural, not human, enforcement, specifically karma.[865] Overall, it is unclear how all these different systems interacted in this polity, and how either the elites or the commoners in this polity interpreted these (often foreign) systems.

[913] Zoroastrianism: in Sasanian Zoroastrianism, "all offenses against religious norms which could endanger the soul of the delinquent [...] were subject to punishment by religious authorities." [914] Buddhism: main expectation appears to have been of supernatural, not human, enforcement, specifically karma.[915] Overall, it is unclear how all these different systems interacted in this polity, and how either the elites or the commoners in this polity interpreted these (often foreign) systems.
♠ loss/seizure of property or wealth ♣ suspected unknown ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [916], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. Greek religion/ideology: a number of laws, traditions and regulations were linked to the protection of moral values[917], but many of these do not appear to be sufficiently religion-based for our interests. Hinduism: main expectation appears to have been of supernatural, not human, enforcement, specifically through reincarnation. [918] Zoroastrianism: in Sasanian Zoroastrianism, "all offenses against religious norms which could endanger the soul of the delinquent [...] were subject to punishment by religious authorities." There are references to city councils and some cities had a city seal."[943]

♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ inferred present ♥ "The gold weight standard of approximately eight grams corresponds to that of Roman coins of the 1st century. Gold bullion from Rome would be melted and used for the Kushan mints, into three denominations: the double state, the state, and the quarter stater (or dinara)."[948] "Copper portrait coins appear in the reign of Kujula, and gold currency, in standardized weights, appears under the empire's second king, Vima. This standard remains constant for more than two centuries, suggesting a high degree of control of currency (Rosenfeld 1967:20)."[949]

Patronage

♠ within-class formal patronage ♣ ♥

♠ within-class informal patronage ♣ inferred present ♥ During the Kushan Empire, "women who were members of artisan and small-scale land owning families, were also recorded as donors, particularly to Buddhist and Jain institutions."[958] Inscription evidence for non-royal donations to religious institutions.[959] Kushan ruling elite made donations to shrines.[960] During the Kushan Empire, "women who were members of artisan and small-scale land owning families, were also recorded as donors, particularly to Buddhist and Jain institutions."[961]
♠ between-class informal patronage ♣ inferred present ♥ Kushans sponsored Buddhist art.[962] No official religion but Buddhism was the dominant religion and received the most patronage.[963] "There is no doubt that the Kushan era was the Golden Age of Buddhist art, and under Kanishka, Gandhara became a holy land, a jewel of Buddhist civilization."[964] "Kanishka II was, without doubt, a great protector of Buddhism and founded monasteries and built stupas... [he convened] the Buddhist synod in Kashmir, a decisive turning-point in the life of the Buddhist schools. According to tradition, this synod of the Sarvastivada school compiled the Jnanaprasthanam and entrusted Asvaghosa, the famous poet, with providing for the correct language form of the commentary written by Katyayana. Essentially, his charge was to rewrite the Buddhist works in Sanskrit."[965]

Public Goods[edit]

♠ provision of alimentary supplementation ♣ ♥

♠ Drinking water ♣ ♥ According to an inscription from Dilberjin the stronghold and sanctuary there "the stronghold was at first provided with water from a source lying outside the walls where later a sardoba was built. When the water of the source began to fail, a well was dug in the bastion flanking the gate and the use of the water was strictly regulated. These and other measures were apparently taken by order of King Vima Kadphises."[966] inscription seems to refer to pure running water conducted from an old spring to the sanctuary.

♠ provision of drinking water ♣ ♥

♠ Irrigation works ♣ present ♥ Irrigation canals were constructed on large scale. "As a result of the extensive development of irrigation networks, practically all the main provinces of Central Asia were brought under cultivation during this period and the establishment of the major crop-growing oases was completed."[967] "In the K’ang-chü-Kushan period, when irrigation systems reached their highest level of development, the area under irrigation along the lower reaches of the Amu Darya and Syr Darya totalled 35,000-38,000 km2 (13,000 km2 on the lower Amu Darya and 22,000-25,000 km2 on the lower Syr Darya). Thus, in antiquity, the land area under irrigation along the lower reaches of the Amu Darya and the Syr Darya was four times greater than it is today. ... not more than 10-15 per cent of the land area, the irrigation zone, was directly used for crop-raising, in spite of the substantial supply of water."[968] "The process of carrying water to the fields was improved and various water distribution devices were introduced. Irrigation was effected in accordance with a specific flow pattern: main river, head, main canal, distribution canal, irrigation canal and fields. The total length of one of the largest canals of the period, known as the ancient Kirkkiz canal (right-bank Chorasmia), was 90 km."[969] "The major achievements of Kushan irrigation engineering included the boring of tunnel-like water-intake channels at the heads of main canals that emerged from the sheer rock sides of a mountain river, and the construction of aqueducts across ravines or gaps in mountain ridges."[970]

Entertainment and Knowledge Well-Being[edit]

♠ Funding for festivals ♣ inferred present ♥ During the Kushan Period sangharamas were involved in "celebration of festivals or for other gatherings of a social nature."[971] Kushan elite made frequent donations to the religions and so must have either directly or indirectly funded religious festivals.

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ inferred present ♥ According to the Milindapanha cities an ideal city would contain "parks, pleasances, lakes, lotus-pools".[972] In cities "Here and there street actors, conjurers and acrobats gave performances, or professional wrestlers were locked in combat."[973] Boating lake? Depends how broadly entertainment buildings are defined. A walk in a landscaped park is a form of entertainment. Entertainment specialists existed.

♠ Theatres ♣ inferred present ♥ "In the ruins of a Buddhist monument near Swat in Pakistan, archaeologists have found a series of sixteen carved stone panels that made up the risers of a stair-case leading to the main stupa, a tower containing the relics of the Buddha. Corinthian columns with acanthus capitals suggest that the figures are actors in a Greek-style theater."[974] The figures wear Roman-like belted tunics but trousers like horseman and also conical hats which were "a signature of the early nomads". "So what are we looking at? A Kushan group wearing Roman clothing and playing Greek music?"[975] There have been no archaeological discoveries of theatres but Kushan interest in Greek drama would suggest they at least had stages for performances. Entertainment specialists existed.
♠ Arenas ♣ inferred absent ♥ no mention of sporting arenas in the literature.
♠ Ritual buildings/spaces ♣ present ♥ Cities contained temples, sancturies and religious statues.[976] Shrines and cemeteries.[977] Surkh Kota noted for its dynastic temples "set up during the reign of the Great Kushan king Kanishka and is successors in the second century A.D."[978] "Hadda was the magnificent Buddhist centre of Gandhara about 8km south of Jalalabad. Like a golden mirage of towers, the thousand stupas of Nagarahara and Hadda drew the Chinese pilgrims Fa-hsien and Hsuan-tsang to this pilgrimage centre in the fifth and seventh centuries A.D. respectively."[979]
♠ Other ♣ ♥
♠ accessibility of entertainment buildings ♣ ♥

♠ Full-time entertainment specialists ♣ present ♥ In cities "Here and there street actors, conjurers and acrobats gave performances, or professional wrestlers were locked in combat."[980] Buddhist art depicts musicians, playing instruments such as the harp, drum[981] whilst the Kushan period Sadharmapundarika (Lotus Sutra) mentions conch trumpets, lutes, reed pipes, flutes of sugarcane and cymbals.[982] Possibly dancers. Buddhist art depicts Indian girls dancing in Hellenistic style.[983]

♠ status of full-time entertainment specialists ♣ ♥

♠ Access to education ♣ present ♥ "The monastery served as a flourishing place for Buddhist education (Smith. 262)."[984] Buddhist education in India during the subsequent Gupta empire could provide some indication of education during the Kushan period: a student was taught in a "Vihara" monastic school or a Matha. "There were two classes of lay pupils. The former called Manava (child), chiefly read the Buddhist scriptures with the intention of receiving ordination at some future date, while the latter called brahmacharin (student), read the secular books alone without any intention of quitting the world. Unlike the novice who was maintained by the common fund of the Samgha, the lay pupils had to provide for their own expenses."[985] In the Kushan Period sangharamas became a place for training Buddhist monks "for educating the masses and for missionary work abroad".[986]

♠ Publicly funded or subsidized education ♣ inferred absent ♥ there was no large state institution that could have administered and funded an education system at least at the level of the state in Gandhara. Education appears to have been done by the religions.
♠ Schools for children? ♣ inferred present ♥ Buddhist education in India during the subsequent Gupta empire could provide some indication of education during the Kushan period: a student was taught in a "Vihara" monastic school or a Matha. "There were two classes of lay pupils. The former called Manava (child), chiefly read the Buddhist scriptures with the intention of receiving ordination at some future date, while the latter called brahmacharin (student), read the secular books alone without any intention of quitting the world. Unlike the novice who was maintained by the common fund of the Samgha, the lay pupils had to provide for their own expenses."[987] In the Kushan Period sangharamas became a place for training Buddhist monks "for educating the masses and for missionary work abroad".[988]
♠ Universities, “proto-universities”, or other higher education ♣ inferred present ♥ "Tolstov, in his study of the remains of the ancient irrigation works in Chorasmia, noted that it was precisely during the period of antiquity that a school of irrigation engineers and high priests of science emerged at Chorasmia; it remained in existence until the time of Qutayba's campaign against Khwarizm (ancient Chorasmia). The school included experts in mathematics, water engineering, cartography, astronomy and calendrical observations, which were of great importance for an extensive irrigation economy."[989] Part of Chorasmia region is within boundaries of Kushan Empire. "There were some important Buddhist centres in Bactria, at Termcz and Ayrtam, where missionary work of both the Mahasanghika and the Sarvastivada schools was active."[990] "When Fah-hien visited Nalanda in c.410 A.D., there was no educational activity at the place but by the end of the fifth century this Buddhist monastery became a famous university."[991] "Though from medieval times until very recent years, the art of music and dancing were looked as quite unfit for respectable Indian girls and were only practiced by lowcaste women and prostitutes, this was not the case in ancient days when well-to-do girls were taught singing and dancing as well as other lady like arts, such as painting and garland making. As for the rest of the womankind they were uneducated."[992] University of Valabhi promoted Brahmanic education. "It came into existence in the later part of the Gupta age" and existed for about six centuries. Nalanda is not within the Kushan polity region but this reference may provide some context to the extent of higher education in India during Kushan times. According to Fa-hien, Taxila had no educational importance under the Saka kings at the beginning of the 5th CE (i.e. it once did have some importance).[993] According to Fa-hien, the following monasteries were well-known centres of religious and secular education (but not the only ones): Udyan, Suvastu, Gandhara, Takshashila, Purushapura, Nagarhara, Mathura, Samkashya, Kanyakubja, Kosala, Jetvana Vihara, Kapilavastu, Ramgrama, Kushinagara, Vaisali, Pataliputra, Rajgriha, Gaya, Varanasi, Kausambi, Champa and Tamralipta."[994] list includes cities within Kushan Empire e.g. Gandhara, Mathura, Purushapura etc. In the Kushan empire sangharamas emerged that focussed on specialized activities (rather than religious rituals) which had (as at Dharmarajika and Takht-i-Bahi) scriptoriums, assembly halls, libraries and training institutes and attracted visiting scholars.[995]
♠ Libraries ♣ present ♥ In the Kushan empire sangharamas emerged that focussed on specialized activities (rather than religious rituals) which had (as at Dharmarajika and Takht-i-Bahi) scriptoriums, assembly halls, libraries and training institutes and attracted visiting scholars.[996] "Volumes after volumes of texts were compiled for the use of thousands of members of the Buddhist clergy and for missionary work. Well-stocked libraries emerged in each sangharama, which served to educate the resident trainee-onks as well as visiting scholars."[997]
♠ Museums ♣ ♥
♠ Literacy rate ♣ [20-35] ♥ Sivaism, Vishnuism, Jainism, Buddhism - and their different schools "penetrated Central Asia" from India where there was Zoroastrianism, Greek divinities, pre-Zoroastrian cults, and ancient Iranian religions of the horseriding nomads.[998] In Gupta India Buddhist mathas provided education both to the poor and to the children of the rich.[999] In Gupta era India "There was no arrangement of educating the Sudras, the outcaste caused by intercaste marriage, the tribal people and the untouchable class. Hence there was no literacy among them. But in Buddhist institutions no distinction was made according to Varnas of classes as in Brahmanic system."[1,000] In Gupta era India "the better class of women seem to have been educated and there are several references in the works of Sanskrit poetry and drama by women authors of which some fragments survive."[1,001] "Though from medieval times until very recent years, the art of music and dancing were looked as quite unfit for respectable Indian girls and were only practiced by lowcaste women and prostitutes, this was not the case in ancient days when well-to-do girls were taught singing and dancing as well as other lady like arts, such as painting and garland making. As for the rest of the womankind they were uneducated."[1,002] Kushans - yes, men and women. Priests - yes. Merchants - yes, men and women. Peasants - low.

Prosocial Behavior[edit]

♠ Volunteering ♣ ♥

♠ Charity ♣ present ♥ "When monasteries grew into large institutions, they provided hospitality to traveling traders."[1,003] During the Kushan Empire, "women who were members of artisan and small-scale land owning families, were also recorded as donors, particularly to Buddhist and Jain institutions."[1,004] presumably this hospitality was paid for directly or indirectly? Voluntary Associations

♠ voluntary trade associations ♣ present ♥ In India: "The heads of the guilds were noted by a number of terms: pramukha (chief), mahattama (head man), jyesthaka (senior). According to the inscriptions, an elder was known as the sresthin (best one). In theory, only a person who had achieved the highest level of skill in his trade could become an elder. The guild heads had their own personal seals bearing their name and the title of sresthin; they were assisted by agents and a secretary (kayastha). The guild heads regulated working conditions and rates of pay. In consultation with the heads of other guilds, they raised or lowered their prices, depending on circumstances."[1,006]
♠ prestige of voluntary trade associations ♣ equal ♥ In India: the guild head "was in charge of security and had a special armed detachment to protect guild property and funds and to escort caravans. The guilds probably had special premises for their administrators, and special banners and ceremonial badges that members work on festive occasions."[1,007] "Some guilds were very rich and possessed real-estate, including some buildings and large temple-complexes."[1,008] "The head of the guild enjoyed high social status and was sometimes a dignitary of the royal court. The state supported the guilds and protected their rights and property. In written sources rulers are warned not to interfere with the customs of the guilds, and to confirm their status. The ruler should only interfere if their usages and procedures were violated"[1,009] sometimes a dignitary of the royal court

This control enabled them to have a share in the profits in the lucrative trade between Rome and China."[1,014]

This standard remains constant for more than two centuries, suggesting a high degree of control of currency (Rosenfeld 1967:20)."[1,017] Silk Route: "the Kushans were able to exercise a certain degree of control over the goods passing through this route. This control enabled them to have a share in the profits in the lucrative trade between Rome and China."[1,018]

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ ♥ Main sources of evidence are coins and inscriptions on "structures, boulders, Buddhist and Jain images and architectural complexes, portrait sculptures, and seals."[1,021] "Virtually nothing is known about taxation and revenue".[1,022] "Indian theoreticians of the pre-Kushana age (and also of the Kushana period) seem to have been of the opinion that taxation should not destroy the substance of the people, and that taxes should be levied by slow, almost imperceptible manner..."[1,023]

Taxes are imposed on:

♠ Land ♣ inferred present ♥ "Virtually nothing is known about taxation and revenue".[1,024] Archives from Old Nisa 1st century BCE may provide some indication for the different types of land tax that might have existed: uzbar land (levy, in Achaemenid period was revenue directly received from royal land), dastkirt royal estates, patbazik land within royal estates (which in Achaemenid times required a "delivery to the king of a contribution in kind").[1,025]
♠ Property ♣ ♥ "Virtually nothing is known about taxation and revenue".[1,026]
♠ People ♣ present ♥ In the agricultural communes the karikar (craftsmen) paid a poll-tax.[1,027]
♠ Trade ♣ inferred present ♥ "Copper portrait coins appear in the reign of Kujula, and gold currency, in standardized weights, appears under the empire's second king, Vima. This standard remains constant for more than two centuries, suggesting a high degree of control of currency (Rosenfeld 1967:20)."[1,028] Kushans "made the Silk Routes possible".[1,029] Silk Route: "the Kushans were able to exercise a certain degree of control over the goods passing through this route. This control enabled them to have a share in the profits in the lucrative trade between Rome and China."[1,030]
♠ Income ♣ ♥ "Virtually nothing is known about taxation and revenue".[1,031]
♠ Production ♣ ♥ "Virtually nothing is known about taxation and revenue".[1,032]

Taxes are imposed by:

♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ inferred present ♥ Kushans sought to control the Silk Route and apparently greatly profited from it: "the Kushans were able to exercise a certain degree of control over the goods passing through this route. This control enabled them to have a share in the profits in the lucrative trade between Rome and China."[1,033] This implies there were taxes on trade which were directly imposed by the ruling Kushans.
♠ local elites ♣ inferred present ♥ "The inscriptions mention two terms - 'gramika' and 'padrapala' - both signifying 'village headman', who collected the king's dues and took cognizance of crimes in his area. There is no information about the local government that we find later in the Gupta period."[1,034] Kushan rule appears to have been highly decentralized and likely had different tax systems in different regions where local elites would have imposed taxes. It is known that tributes were paid by local rulers.[1,035].
♠ state officials ♣ inferred present ♥ "Virtually nothing is known about taxation and revenue".[1,036] The extent of any state beyond that of the royal court and the Buddhist establishment in Gandhara is not well known. While there were 'state officials' at regional level government that would have imposed taxes these are coded under local elites. A state chancery did exist which used used both "Bactrian written in Greek script and Gandhari written in Kharoshthi".[1,037]


Type of taxes paid:

♠ Staple ♣ inferred present ♥ "Virtually nothing is known about taxation and revenue".[1,038] Kushan rule appears to have been highly decentralized and likely had different tax systems in different regions. It is very likely that taxes would have been paid in staples somewhere within the empire, if not in the core region of Gandhara.
♠ Labor ♣ ♥ "Virtually nothing is known about taxation and revenue".[1,039] Kushan rule appears to have been highly decentralized and likely had different tax systems in different regions, so labor taxation is a possibility.
♠ Currency ♣ inferred present ♥ Currency existed: "Copper portrait coins appear in the reign of Kujula, and gold currency, in standardized weights, appears under the empire's second king, Vima. This standard remains constant for more than two centuries, suggesting a high degree of control of currency (Rosenfeld 1967:20)."[1,040] Kushans sought to control the Silk Route and apparently greatly profited from it: "the Kushans were able to exercise a certain degree of control over the goods passing through this route. This control enabled them to have a share in the profits in the lucrative trade between Rome and China."[1,041] These two facts imply there were taxes on trade which could potentially be paid in widespread currency.

Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ ♥ The Mahayana school of Buddhism became popular during the Kushan period because the "unprecedently rich" society found the doctrine of "eliminating material desire" difficult to understand but they easily grapsed the concept of "making payments for religious merits" which the doctrine encouraged.[1,042] Thus "Newly affluent Buddhist monasteries came to possess many material things, including grandiose stupas and monastic buildings."[1,043] "In Buddhist monasteries at Shaikhan Dheri, a site in the Gandharan region, archeologists have found many wine storage jars and distillation equipment ... suggesting that Buddhist monasteries were producing liquor, even though such activities were contrary to their centuries-old monastic discipline."[1,044] "Buddhist monasteries became large economic enterprises, engaging in all kinds of business, including trading, investing, and the making of alcohol."[1,045] "To make a monastery look like a paradise, the monks festooned stupas and buildings with silk and gems."[1,046]

♠ staple cost of principal source of carbohydrates ♣ ♥

♠ Top fortune ♣ ♥

♠ Other wealth distribution ♣ ♥

HPI Variables[edit]

♠ Dedicated police force ♣ inferred absent ♥ The security forces run by the guilds appear to be both guards and military troops. In Kushan India guilds may have played a significant role in town policing. “It is possible that the organization of the guilds was not so formalized in Central Asia as it was in India.” “The guild heads had their own personal seals bearing their name and the title of sresthin; they were assisted by agents and a secretary (kayastha). ... The head was in charge of security and had a special armed detachment to protect guild property and funds and to escort caravans. The guilds probably had special premises for their administrators…” “The state supported the guilds and protected their rights and property.” “From the text of the Arthasastra (XI.1.4) some scholars are of the opinion that the guilds provided soldiers. It is clear that armed detachments, who protected guild property in peace-time, were placed at the disposal of the state during war. It is also known from the epics that the guilds were regarded as one of the pillars of state authority.”[1,047] “The dandanayaka was presumably the wielder of the rod (dandayaka), acting both as commissioner of police to prevent crime and as a judge or criminal magistrate administering justice.”[1,048] The Kushans used an Indian title Dandanayaka which denoted a military and civil status. B N Puri suggested they were appointed chiefs who raised forces and carried out the role of judge and maintained law and order in their regions. H P Chakraborty noted 'the names of higher grade officers of the Kushanas show that they were all foreign’. "What puzzles us is the question why the Kusanas introduced a term designating one kind of officials which is essentially Indian. This at once arouses doubt in the mind of scholars that the word perhaps appeared in the administrative machinery before the advent of the Kusanas. But it is interesting to note that the term was neither familiar to the Bactrian Greeks nor was it found in Kautilya's Arthasastra nor even in the list of the officers created by Asoka.”[1,049]
♠ Police levels ♣ 4 ♥ King - dandanayaka - (commissioner of police) - Guild head - Security detachments. The security forces run by the guilds appear to be both guards and military troops. In Kushan India guilds may have played a significant role in town policing. “It is possible that the organization of the guilds was not so formalized in Central Asia as it was in India.” “The guild heads had their own personal seals bearing their name and the title of sresthin; they were assisted by agents and a secretary (kayastha). ... The head was in charge of security and had a special armed detachment to protect guild property and funds and to escort caravans. The guilds probably had special premises for their administrators…” “The state supported the guilds and protected their rights and property.” “From the text of the Arthasastra (XI.1.4) some scholars are of the opinion that the guilds provided soldiers. It is clear that armed detachments, who protected guild property in peace-time, were placed at the disposal of the state during war. It is also known from the epics that the guilds were regarded as one of the pillars of state authority.”[1,050] “The dandanayaka was presumably the wielder of the rod (dandayaka), acting both as commissioner of police to prevent crime and as a judge or criminal magistrate administering justice.”[1,051] The Kushans used an Indian title Dandanayaka which denoted a military and civil status. B N Puri suggested they were appointed chiefs who raised forces and carried out the role of judge and maintained law and order in their regions. H P Chakraborty noted 'the names of higher grade officers of the Kushanas show that they were all foreign’. "What puzzles us is the question why the Kusanas introduced a term designating one kind of officials which is essentially Indian. This at once arouses doubt in the mind of scholars that the word perhaps appeared in the administrative machinery before the advent of the Kusanas. But it is interesting to note that the term was neither familiar to the Bactrian Greeks nor was it found in Kautilya's Arthasastra nor even in the list of the officers created by Asoka.”[1,052]
♠ Proscriptions of elites and or property ♣ ♥ Use of the state to repress or purge rival elite groups.
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ ♥


♠ Military control over independent or semi-independent vassal states ♣ present ♥ "Probably Mathura was also established as a vassal State of the Kushan Empire during this period.” Vima Taktu (81-100 CE) and Vima Kadphises (101-127 CE)[1,053] In the regions, rule was likely through indigenous institutions through vassal kings or appointed trustworthy leaders, with Satraps known.[1,054] Thus when kingdoms could not be ruled as vassals (like Kashgar during the reign of Kanishka and Mathura during the reigns of Huvishka and Vasudeva) local uprisings occurred.[1,055] Satrapies (powerful governors) who possessed a well-equipped military might have been a more effective system for ruling the regions but "putting in place an effective satrapy system was not within the capabilities of the Kushans."[1,056]
